إِنَّ مَا فِيْهِ إِظْهَارُ الْجَزَع وَالتَّأَسُّفِ فَهُوَ مِنْ كَلامِ النَّاسِ . ترجمہ: بلاشبہ ہر وہ فعل جس میں جزع اور افسوس کا اظہار ہوتو یہ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہوگا۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ کام نماز میں جو کلام الناس کے مشابہ ہو تو ایسے کلام سے نماز باطل ہو جاتی ہے، مذکورہ اصول کو صاحب ہدایہ ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر کوئی آدمی نماز میں آہ کرتا ہے یا بلند آواز سے روتا ہے اور اس کا یہ رونا در دیا تکلیف یا کسی اور مصیبت کی وجہ سے ہو تو اس سے نماز باطل ہو جائے گی ، وجہ یہ کہ جب اس کا رونا درد وغیرہ کی وجہ سے ہے تو یہ کلام الناس میں سے ہوا، اور کلام الناس فی الصلاۃ سے نماز باطل ہو جاتی ہے، لہذا اس قسم کے رونے سے بھی نماز باطل ہو جائے گی ۔ الجوهرة النيرة : كتاب الصلاة، باب صفة الصلاة، ج ا ص ۶۵، ط: المطبعة الخيرية ] الهداية كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها، ج ۱ ص ۱۳۷، ط: رحمانيه فَإِنْ أَنَّ فِي صَلَاتِهِ أَوْ تَأَوَّهَ أَوْ بَكَى فَارْتَفَعَ بُكَاؤُهُ أَى حَصَلَ بِهِ حُرُوفٌ إِنْ كَانَ مِنْ ذِكْرِ الْجَنَّةِ أَوْ النَّارِ لَمْ يَضُرَّهُ، لأَنَّهُ يَدُلُّ عَلَى زِيَادَةِ الْخُشُوعِ فَكَانَ فِي مَعْنَى التَّسْبِيحِ، وَالْبُكَاءُ فِي الصَّلَاةِ مِنْ خَوْفِ اللَّهِ لَا يَقْطَعُ الصَّلَاةَ، لِأَنَّ اللَّهَ تَعَالَى مَدَحَ عَلَى ذَلِكَ فَقَالَ (وَيَخِرُّونَ لِلْأَذْقَانِ يَبْكُونَ) أَى يَخِرُّونَ سُجُودًا عَلَى الْوُجُوهِ يَكُونُ وَالْمُرَادُ بِالأذْقَانِ الْوُجُوهُ، وَعَنْ رَسُولِ اللَّهِ صَلَّى اللَّهُ عَلَيْهِ وَسَلَّمَ أَنَّهُ كَانَ يُصَلَّى فَيُسْمَعُ لِصَدْرِهِ أَزِيزٌ كَأَزِيزِ الْمِرْجَلِ مِنْ الْبُكَاءِ وَعَنْ عَبْدِ اللَّهِ بْنِ شَدَّادٍ قَالَ كُنت أُصَلَّى خَلْفَ عُمَرَ رَضِيَ اللَّهُ عَنْهُ صَلَاةَ الصُّبْحِ وَكَانَ يَقْرَأُ سُورَةَ يُوسُفَ حَتَّى إِذَا بَلَغَ قَالَ إِنَّمَا أَشْكُو بَنِّي وَحُزْنِى إِلَى اللَّهِ سَمِعْت نَشِيجَهُ وَأَنَا آخِرُ الصُّفُوفِ الْأَزِيزُ غَلَيَانُ الْقِدْرِ وَالْمِرْجَلُ الْقِدْرُ وَإِنْ كَانَ مِنْ وَجَعٍ أَوْ مُصِيبَةٍ قَطَعَ الصَّلَاةَ، لِأَنَّ فِيهِ إِظْهَارَ الْجَزَعِ وَالتَّأَسُّفِ فَكَانَ مِنْ كَلامِ النَّاسِ.
Indeed, anything that shows distress and regret is from the speech of people. Translation (Urdu): Every action that shows distress and regret will be from the category of human speech. Explanation: The meaning of this principle is that any action in prayer that resembles human speech invalidates the prayer. After mentioning this principle, the author of Hidayah proves that if a person moans in prayer or cries loudly, and his crying is due to pain or a calamity, then his prayer is invalidated. The reason is that when his crying is due to pain etc., it is from human speech, and human speech in prayer invalidates it. Therefore, this type of crying also invalidates the prayer. Al-Jawhara al-Nayyira: Kitab al-Salah, Bab sifat al-salah, vol. 1, p. 65, al-Matba'a al-Khayriyya; Al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma yufsidu al-salah wa ma yukrahu fiha, vol. 1, p. 137, Rahmaniyyah: So if he moans in his prayer, or says 'ah', or cries and his crying intensifies, i.e., letters are produced, if it is from the mention of Jannah or the Fire, it does not harm him, because it indicates increase in khushu', so it is like tasbih. And crying in prayer from fear of Allah does not break the prayer, because Allah Almighty praised it when He said, 'And they fall down on their faces weeping' (Quran 17:109) — meaning they fall prostrate on their faces, and 'azqaan' refers to faces. And from the Messenger of Allah, peace be upon him, that he used to pray and a sound like the boiling of a pot could be heard from his chest due to crying. And from Abdullah ibn Shaddad: I used to pray behind Umar (may Allah be pleased with him) the Fajr prayer and he would recite Surah Yusuf; when he reached 'He said, I only complain of my grief and sorrow to Allah' I heard his crying while I was in the last rows. And if it is from pain or calamity, it breaks the prayer because it shows distress and regret, so it is from human speech.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This is a ruling with proof distinguishing between valid and invalid crying in prayer. The principle states that any action showing distress and regret (jaza' and ta'assuf) is like ordinary human speech and thus invalidates prayer. The commentator explains that if a person cries due to worldly pain or calamity, this expresses distress, which is analogous to speaking. Therefore, the prayer is invalidated. The text contrasts this with crying from fear of Allah, which is praised and does not invalidate. The proof includes the Quranic verse praising those who weep in fear, and the hadith of the Prophet's weeping. However, when the cause is worldly, the action is like kalam al-nas (human speech), which breaks the prayer. This distinction is crucial in Hanafi fiqh.

Key Terms
جَزَع (jazaʿ): Distress and anxiety in the face of worldly affliction, which is considered like human speech in prayer.
تَأَسُّف (taʾassuf): Regret and sorrow expressed verbally or non-verbally, treated as kalam al-nas.
كَلَام النَّاس (kalām al-nās): Ordinary human speech or actions that convey messages, which invalidate prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
A person cries in prayer because of a headache. According to the principle from the Hidayah, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is valid because crying is natural.
B) The prayer is invalid because it shows distress, like human speech.
C) The prayer is only invalid if the crying is loud.
D) The prayer is invalid only if the person speaks words.

Key Takeaway
Crying from worldly distress invalidates prayer; crying from fear of Allah does not.

The Two-Letter Word in Prayer: Abu Yusuf's Position
Intra-Hanafi dispute on two-letter words in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the intra-Hanafi ruling on two-letter words in prayer and distinguish between extra and original letters.

Core Idea
A two-letter word invalidates prayer only if both letters are original — Abu Yusuf's more strict view.

When a person speaks during prayer, the ruling depends on what is said. This lesson examines a subtle yet critical distinction: whether a two-letter word contains extra letters (ḥurūf zāʾida) or original letters (ḥurūf aṣliyya). Imam Abu Yusuf held that if both letters are original, the prayer becomes invalid, while the other founding imams of the Hanafi school considered such words permissible. Understanding this difference is essential for knowing when a prayer is nullified.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Utterance
Determine the word uttered and count its letters. For a two-letter word, note each letter individually.
e.g., 'qad' (قَد) = qāf and dāl.
2
Classify Each Letter as Extra or Original
Decide whether each letter is a ḥarf zāʾid (extra, common in prepositions/particles) or ḥarf aṣlī (original, part of a three‑letter root).
Extra letters are often found in prepositions like 'bi' (ب) and particles like 'hal' (هَل)? Actually 'hal' is two letters but considered original? See tradition.
3
Apply the Rule
If both letters or one is extra, the prayer remains valid. If both are original, then according to Abu Yusuf the prayer is invalidated.
The rule hinges on the nature of the letters, not the number of letters alone.
4
Note the Intra‑Madhhab Ikhtilāf
Recognize that this is Abu Yusuf's position; Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al‑Shaybānī are reported to hold that even two‑letter original words do not invalidate prayer.
The text does not provide a tarjīḥ, but the majority position may be the more lenient one.
5
Practical Consequence (Thamarah)
If a person says 'qad' (قَد) in prayer, according to Abu Yusuf the prayer must be repeated; according to the other two it is valid.
This difference matters in real‑life congregations and individual rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ الْكَلِمَةَ إِذَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَى حَرُفَيْنِ وَهُمَا زَائِدَتَانِ أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا لَا تَفْسُدُ الصَّلَاةُ وَإِنْ كَانَتَا أَصَلِيَّتَيْنِ تَفْسُدُ عِنْدَ أَبِى يُوْسُفَ رَحِمَهُ اللَّهُ تَعَالَى . ترجمہ: کلمہ جب دو حرفوں پر مشتمل ہو اور وہ دونوں زائد ہوں، یا ان میں سے ایک زائد ہوتو نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی اور اگر وہ دونوں اصلی ہوں تو امام ابو یوسف رحمہ اللہ کے نزدیک نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ جب کلمہ دو حرفوں پر مشتمل ہو اور وہ دونوں حرف زوائد میں سے ہوں ، یا ان میں سے ایک حرف زوائد میں سے ہو تو نماز فاسد نہیں ہوگی اور اگر دونوں حروف اصلیہ ہوں تو نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی۔ وجہ اس کی یہ ہے کہ کلام عرب کی بنیاد تین حروف پر ہے ۔
Indeed, a word that consists of two letters, if both are extra letters or one of them is extra, the prayer is not invalidated. And if both are original letters, then according to Abu Yusuf (may Allah have mercy on him), the prayer is invalidated. Translation (Urdu): When a word consists of two letters, if both are extra or one is extra, the prayer does not become invalid; but if both are original, then according to Imam Abu Yusuf the prayer becomes invalid. Explanation: The meaning of the above principle is that when a word consists of two letters, and both letters are from the extra letters, or one of them is extra, the prayer is not invalidated; but if both letters are original, the prayer becomes invalid. The reason is that the basis of Arabic speech is three letters.
Source pages p. 112

Commentary
This is an intra-Hanafi dispute concerning the effect of uttering a two-letter word in prayer. The principle states that if a word has two letters, and both are extra (za'id) or one is extra, the prayer remains valid. But if both are original (asli), then according to Imam Abu Yusuf, the prayer is invalidated. The commentator explains that Arabic speech is fundamentally based on three-letter roots (for verbs and nouns). Two-letter words that are made of original letters (like 'qad' which is actually a two-letter particle but considered as speech) are considered as human speech and thus invalidate prayer. However, two-letter words containing extra letters (like 'hal' or 'bi' which are prepositions) are not considered full speech and do not invalidate. Abu Yusuf's position is more strict regarding original two-letter words. The general Hanafi position (Abu Hanifa and Muhammad) might differ, but the text presents Abu Yusuf's view explicitly.

Key Terms
حَرْف زَائِد (ḥarf zāʾid): An extra letter that is not part of the original root, commonly found in prepositions and particles.
حَرْف أَصْلِيّ (ḥarf aṣlī): An original letter that forms part of the root of a word, typically derived from three-letter roots.
كَلِمَة (kalimah): A word; in this context, a two-letter utterance assessed for its effect on prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Yusuf, if a person says 'qad' (قد) in prayer, what is the ruling?
A) The prayer is valid because 'qad' is a two-letter word.
B) The prayer is invalid because 'qad' consists of two original letters.
C) The prayer is invalid only if the word is used in a negative context.
D) The prayer is valid because extra letters are involved.

Key Takeaway
A two-letter word with both letters original invalidates prayer according to Abu Yusuf; otherwise it does not.

What Invalidates Prayer: Original vs Extra Letters
Defines what constitutes human speech that invalidates prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi rule distinguishing original and extra letters to determine whether a spoken word invalidates prayer.

Core Idea
Two original letters = speech = invalidates prayer; extra letters do not.

During salah, speaking human words deliberately invalidates the prayer. But what counts as 'speech'? This passage from Al-Hidayah lays out a precise linguistic criterion: the presence of alphabet letters and the conveyance of meaning. Imam Abu Hanifa further refines this: only words with two original letters (asli) are considered complete speech; words with extra letters (za'id) are not. This distinction relies on Arabic morphology and a famous mnemonic for extra letters.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal principle
The passage states: speech in common custom depends on letters and meaning. Then it gives the specific rule for invalidation: a word with two letters invalidates only if both are original; if one or both are extra, it does not.
This is a qiyas-based derivation from the three-letter root structure of Arabic.
2
Understand the linguistic basis
The author explains that Arabic words are built from three letters: one to begin, one to stop, and one to separate. A single letter is not speech. Two letters are considered like one if extra (looking to the original), but if both are original, they constitute the majority (two-thirds) and stand for the whole.
The concept of 'majority standing for the whole' is a principle in Hanafi usul.
3
Apply the rule to examples
The passage gives two examples: 'Ah' (آه) does not invalidate because both letters are extra (from the mnemonic set). 'Awah' (أوة) invalidates because it has more than two letters (or extra over two), and in such cases originality is irrelevant.
Words with three or more letters always invalidate if they convey meaning, regardless of letter origin.
4
Learn the mnemonic for extra letters
The extra letters are collected in the phrase 'al-yawma tansahu' (اليوم تنساه). Each letter in this phrase is considered extra in Arabic morphology. If a word consists entirely of letters from this set, it is not treated as full speech.
This mnemonic is unique to Hanafi usul al-fiqh.
5
Recognize the epistemological weight
This ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah and is the mufta bihi (operative) position. The derivation is based on qiyas, and the rule is cross-madhhab disputed (Shafi'is may hold different criteria).
The thamarah al-khilaf: under Shafi'i school, even a single letter might invalidate if it conveys meaning.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ كَلامَ النَّاسِ فِي مُتَفَاهَمِ الْعُرْفِ يَتْبَعُ وُجُودَ حُرُوفِ الْهِجَاءِ وَإِفْهَامَ الْمَعْنَى . الْأَصْلُ عِنْدَهُ أَنَّ الْكَلِمَةَ إِذَا اشْتَمَلَتْ عَلَى حَرْفَيْنِ وَهُمَا زَائِدَتَانِ أَوْ إِحْدَاهُمَا لَا تُفْسِدُ، وَإِنْ كَانَتَا أَصْلِيَّتَيْن تُفْسِدُ، وَهَذَا، لِأَنَّ أَصْلَ كَلامِ الْعَرَبِ ثَلَاثَةُ أَحْرُفٍ لِاحْتِيَاجِهِ إِلَى حَرْفٍ يُبْتَدَأُ بِهِ وَحَرُفٌ يُوقَفُ عَلَيْهِ وَحَرْفٌ يَفْصِلُ بَيْنَهُمَا ، فَالْحَرُفَ الْوَاحِدُ أَقَلُّ الْجُمُلَةِ فَلَا يُطْلَقُ عَلَيْهِ اسْمُ الْكَلَامِ، وَالْحَرُفَانِ إِنْ كَانَ أَحَدُهُمَا مِنْ الزَّوَائِدِ كَذَلِكَ لِأَنَّهُ نَظَرَ إِلَى الْأَصْلِ عَلَى حَرْفٍ وَاحِدٍ، وَأَمَّا إِذَا كَانَتَا أَصْلِيَّتَيْنِ فَقَدْ وُجِدَ الأَكْثَرُ، وَهُوَ يَقُومُ مَقَامَ الْكُلِّ، وَالْحُرُوفُ الزَّوَائِدُ عَلَى مَعْنَى أَنَّ كُلُّ زَائِدٍ لَا بُدَّ وَأَنْ يَكُونَ مِنْهَا لَا عَكْسَهُ جَمَعُوهَا فِي قَوْلِهِمُ الْيَوْمَ تَنْسَاهُ، وَعَلَى هَذَا قَوْلُهُ: آهِ لَا تُفْسِدُ ، لِأَنَّهُمَا مِنْ الزَّوَائِدِ ، وَأَوْةٌ تُفْسِدُ، لأنَّهُ زَائِدٌ عَلَى حَرْفَيْنِ فَإِنَّهُ فِي الزَّوَائِدِ عَلَى حَرْفَيْن لَا يُنظَرُ إلَى الْأَصَالَةِ وَالزِّيَادَةِ.
Indeed, the speech of people in common custom follows the existence of the letters of the alphabet and conveying meaning. The principle according to him (Abu Hanifa) is that when a word contains two letters and they are both extra or one of them, it does not invalidate; if they are both original, it invalidates. This is because the basis of Arabic speech is three letters: due to the need for a letter with which to begin, a letter on which to stop, and a letter to separate between them. So the single letter is the minimum of a sentence, so the name 'speech' is not applied to it. Two letters, if one of them is from the extra letters, are similar because he looks to the original as one letter. As for when both are original, then the majority has been found, and it takes the place of the whole. The extra letters, meaning that every extra letter must be from them (the set), not vice versa. They collected them in their saying 'al-yawma tansahu'. Upon this is based his saying: 'Ah' does not invalidate because both are from the extra letters, and 'Awah' invalidates because it is extra over two letters; for in extra letters over two, one does not look to originality or non-originality.
Source pages p. 113

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah presents a key principle regarding what constitutes 'kalam al-nas' (human speech) that invalidates prayer. The principle states that speech in common custom is defined by two criteria: (1) existence of letters of the alphabet, and (2) conveyance of meaning. Imam Abu Hanifa then derives a rule: a word with two letters invalidates prayer only if both letters are original (asli). If both are extra (za'id) or one is extra, it does not invalidate. The reasoning is rooted in Arabic morphology: the minimal structure of a word is three letters (for beginning, stopping, and separating). A single letter is not considered speech. Two letters are considered like one if extra, but if both are original, the majority (two-thirds of three) is found, which stands for the whole. The extra letters are those collected in the mnemonic 'al-yawma tansahu' (today you forget it) – a well-known Hanafi list. Applications: The word 'Ah' (آه) consists of two extra letters (alif and ha, both from the set of extra letters), so it does not invalidate prayer. The word 'Awah' (أوة) has more than two letters or is considered extra, and it invalidates. The principle shows the Hanafi concern for linguistic precision in determining the effect of speech during salah.

Key Terms
حروف الهجاء (huruf al-hija'): Letters of the alphabet; their existence is necessary for speech to be considered.
زائد (za'id): An extra letter that is not part of the original root of a word.
أصلي (asli): An original letter that is part of the root of a word.
كلام الناس (kalam al-nas): Human speech that invalidates prayer if spoken deliberately.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the principle in this passage, which of the following words invalidates prayer when spoken deliberately during salah?
A) آه (Ah) because it has two letters.
B) أوة (Awah) because it has more than two letters or extra letters.
C) ب (Ba) because it is a single letter.
D) ما (Ma) because it has two letters but both are considered original.

Key Takeaway
A word with two original letters constitutes speech that invalidates prayer; extra letters do not count toward the two-letter threshold.

Customary Speech Invalidates Prayer: Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Intra-Hanafi dispute on what constitutes invalidating speech in prayer

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why customary speech invalidates prayer according to the Hanafi school and evaluate Abu Yusuf's objection.

Core Idea
Any speech common in people's addresses is kalam al-nas and invalidates prayer.

In this lesson, we examine a key principle from Al-Hidayah: that whatever speech is current in people's everyday addresses is considered 'kalam al-nas' and thus invalidates the prayer if spoken intentionally. The passage also addresses a disagreement attributed to Abu Yusuf, who apparently held a more restrictive view. By analyzing this intra-Hanafi dispute, you will understand how the school defines invalidating speech and why the majority view prevails.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position on customary speech
Identify the ruling: any phrase commonly used in people's addresses (mukhatabat al-nas) is considered kalam al-nas and invalidates prayer. The example given is saying 'yarhamuka Allah' in response to a sneeze.
This is the zahir al-riwayah position.
2
Identify Abu Yusuf's objection
Abu Yusuf apparently held that not all such phrases invalidate. The exact basis is not fully stated, but the rebuttal implies he considered the letters in the phrase to be 'extra' (zawaid), possibly arguing that such phrases are not real speech.
His view is deemed 'not strong' (la yaqwa).
3
Examine the Hanafi rebuttal
The passage provides a definition of kalam al-nas: it depends on the existence of alphabet letters (huruf al-hija) and conveyance of meaning. The phrase 'yarhamuka Allah' satisfies both, even if its letters are all considered extra. Thus, it qualifies as kalam al-nas.
The rebuttal uses a talil (reasoning) based on the definition.
4
Analyse the reasoning from the source
The key move: the fact that letters are 'extra' does not prevent them from forming speech. The phrase still consists of letters and communicates meaning, so it falls under the general principle.
This addresses the specific counter-argument of Abu Yusuf.
5
Determine the practical difference (thamarah al-khilaf)
The practical consequence: According to Abu Hanifa, saying 'yarhamuka Allah' in prayer invalidates it. According to Abu Yusuf, it might not. The mufta bihi is Abu Hanifa's view, so the ruling is that such speech does invalidate.
This directly affects the validity of prayer in everyday situations.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
إِنَّ مَا يَجْرِى فِى مُخَاطَبَاتِ النَّاسِ فَهُوَ مِنْ كَلامِهِم. وَقَوْلُ أَبِى يُوسُفَ لَا يَقْوَى لِأَنَّ كَلامَ النَّاسِ فِي مُتَفَاهَمِهِمْ أَى أَهْلِ الْعُرْفِ يَتْبَعُ وُجُودَ حُرُوفِ الْهِجَاءِ وَإِفْهَامَ الْمَعْنَى وَيَتَحَقَّقُ ذَلِكَ فِي حُرُوفٍ كُلُّهَا زَوَائِدُ.
Indeed, whatever occurs in the addresses of people is from their speech. And the saying of Abu Yusuf is not strong because the speech of people in their common understanding – i.e., the people of custom – follows the existence of the letters of the alphabet and conveying meaning, and that is realized in letters that are all extra.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage from Al-Hidayah presents another principle: any speech that is current in people's addresses (i.e., commonly used in conversation) is considered 'kalam al-nas' and thus invalidates prayer if spoken intentionally. The example given in the Urdu commentary (not in the Arabic) is that if a praying person says 'yarhamuka Allah' (may God have mercy on you) in response to a sneeze, it invalidates the prayer because it is customary speech. The Arabic then addresses an objection attributed to Abu Yusuf, who apparently held a different view. The response is that Abu Yusuf's saying is 'not strong' because the definition of kalam al-nas depends on the existence of alphabet letters and conveyance of meaning, and in the case of 'yarhamuka Allah', the letters are all considered extra (or the word is composed of extra letters in terms of the principle). However, the exact details are unclear from this snippet. The overall ruling is that customary speech invalidates prayer, and this is the relied-upon Hanafi position.

Key Terms
مخاطبات الناس (mukhatabat al-nas): Addresses or conversations of people; speech that is commonly used in social interaction.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because it is a Quranic phrase.
B) Because it is commonly used in people's addresses.
C) Because it contains extra letters.
D) Because it is a response to a sneeze.

Key Takeaway
Customary speech invalidates prayer, and the Hanafi definition focuses on common usage, not on the composition of letters.

Salah — Prayer
beginner

Definition of Kalam al-Nas and Its Conditions
Defines kalam al-nas and its invalidation effect

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the two conditions of kalam al-nas according to Hanafi 'urf and determine whether a given utterance invalidates salah.

Core Idea
Speech invalidates prayer only if it consists of articulate letters conveying meaning.

In the Hanafi school, not every sound made during prayer breaks it. The key is the definition of 'kalam al-nas' (human speech) as understood by custom ('urf). This lesson unpacks that definition and shows how it applies to decide what invalidates salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the legal issue
The issue is what counts as 'speech' that invalidates prayer. The Hanafi school defines it based on custom.
Only intentional utterances matter; forgetfulness has different rules.
2
State the definition of kalam al-nas
According to 'urf, kalam al-nas requires two things: (1) articulate letters (huruf al-hijaa) and (2) that those letters convey meaning.
This definition comes from the custom of the people, not a textual definition.
3
Apply the definition – no letters
If a person in prayer makes a sound that has no letters (e.g., a groan or cough), it is not kalam al-nas and does not invalidate.
This is agreed upon by all scholars.
4
Apply the definition – letters without meaning
If a person in prayer says something with letters but no meaning (e.g., random syllables), it is not kalam al-nas and does not invalidate.
Both conditions must be present for invalidation.
5
Conclude the effect on salah
Only intentional utterances that meet both conditions (letters + meaning) are considered mufsid (invalidating) for salah.
This protects the prayer from being nullified by unintentional or meaningless sounds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ترجمہ: بے شک کلام الناس ہونا عرف کی اصطلاح میں تابع ہوتا ہے حروف ہجاء کے پائے جانے اور معنی سمجھانے کے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ عرف عام میں کلام الناس ہونا دو باتوں کے تابع ہے: اول یہ کہ حروف ہجاء پائے جائیں۔ دوم یہ کہ حروف ہجاء مفید معنی بھی ہوں ۔ مذکورہ اصول کو صاحب ہدایہ ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر مصلی کی آواز میں حروف ہجاء ہی نہ ہو تو بالا تفاق یہ مفسد صلاۃ نہیں ہوگا اور اگر مصلی کی آواز میں حروفِ ہجاء تو ہوں لیکن وہ مفید معنی نہ ہوں تب بھی یہ مفسد صلاۃ نہیں ہے۔
Translation: Indeed, kalam al-nas according to 'urf depends on the presence of letters (huruf al-hijaa) and conveying meaning. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that in common 'urf, kalam al-nas depends on two things: first, that letters (huruf al-hijaa) are present; second, that the letters also convey meaning. After mentioning this principle, the author of al-Hidayah proves that if the one praying has no letters in his voice, then by agreement it does not invalidate prayer. And if the one praying has letters but they do not convey meaning, then also it does not invalidate prayer.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents a foundational qawida (principle) defining 'kalam al-nas' (human speech) in the context of what invalidates prayer (mufsid). The text states that speech, according to custom ('urf), requires two conditions: the presence of articulate letters and that those letters convey meaning. The Hanafi school uses this definition to rule that mere sounds without letters, or letters without meaning, are not considered 'speech' that would nullify salah. This is based on the understanding that the ruling of invalidation applies only to meaningful human speech. The commentary, drawn from works like al-Bahr al-Ra'iq, emphasizes that this definition aligns with the custom of the people ('urf al-nas) and serves as a criterion for determining mufsidat al-salah.

Key Terms
كلام الناس (kalam al-nas): Human speech as defined by custom, requiring articulate letters and meaningful content.
مفسد صلاۃ (mufsid al-salah): That which invalidates prayer, such as intentional human speech fulfilling the definition of kalam al-nas.
حروف ہجاء (huruf al-hijaa): The letters of the alphabet; articulate sounds that form words.
عرف (‘urf): Customary practice of the people, used to define what constitutes speech.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this principle, if a praying person makes a sound that has letters but no meaning, does it invalidate the prayer?
A) Yes, because letters are present.
B) Yes, because any sound invalidates.
C) No, because it does not convey meaning.
D) No, but it is makruh.

Key Takeaway
Kalam al-nas in Hanafi fiqh requires both articulate letters and meaningful content; only speech meeting this definition invalidates prayer.

The Ruling on Saying 'Yarhamukallah' During Prayer
Establishes that common conversational speech invalidates prayer and applies it to the sneeze response.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the principle that common conversational speech invalidates prayer to determine whether specific utterances break the prayer.

Core Idea
Any phrase commonly used in everyday conversation, even if religious, invalidates prayer when spoken deliberately.

The principle of 'kalam al-nas' is a key rule in Hanafi fiqh for determining what speech invalidates prayer. While many assume only worldly talk nullifies prayer, this lesson shows that even a phrase like 'yarhamukallah'—a response to a sneeze—is included because it is ordinary conversational speech. Understanding this principle helps avoid common mistakes during salah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its immediate classification
Consider the specific utterance: saying 'yarhamukallah' during prayer. Determine if it is a form of speech (kalam) or something else like dhikr or du'a.
The act is clearly speech, not recitation or silent thought.
2
Classify the speech as kalam al-nas or not
Apply the definition: kalam al-nas is speech that commonly occurs in people's conversations, containing letters and conveying meaning. Check if 'yarhamukallah' meets this: it has the letter kaf (you) and a clear meaning, and it is commonly used.
Custom (urf) determines what is common.
3
Apply the principle that kalam al-nas invalidates prayer
Once classified as kalam al-nas, the ruling is that it invalidates the prayer (mufsid). No exception for religious phrases.
This is the relied-upon position (mufta bihi).
4
Note the intra-madhhab ikhtilaf
Abu Yusuf reportedly did not consider it invalid. However, the text states his view is weak. Understand why: the definition of kalam al-nas is fulfilled.
Abu Hanifa and Muhammad al-Shaybani agree on invalidation.
5
Conclude the mufta bihi ruling
The operative ruling for fatwa: saying 'yarhamukallah' during prayer invalidates it, and the prayer must be repeated.
The thamarah al-khilaf: if Abu Yusuf's view were followed, the prayer would be valid, but it is not relied upon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۸) إِنَّ مَا يَجْرِى فِى مُخَاطَبَاتِ النَّاسِ فَهُوَ مِنْ كَلامِهِم. ترجمہ: ہروہ کلام جولوگوں کے خطابات میں جاری ہوتا ہے تو وہ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہوگا۔ تشریح : مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ہر وہ کلام جولوگوں کے خطابات میں جاری ہو یعنی لوگوں کی بول چال میں جاری ہو تو وہ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہوگا۔ مذکورہ اصول کو صاحب ہدایہ ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں کہ اگر ایک آدمی کو چھینک آئی اور دوسرا آدمی جو نماز پڑھ رہا ہے اُس نے چھینکنے والے کے جواب میں يَرْحَمُكَ الله “ کہا ، تو اس قائل کی نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی ، کیونکہ يَرحَمُكَ اللهُ “ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہے اور کلام الناس مفسد صلاۃ ہے، لہذا یہ بھی مفسد صلاۃ ہوگا۔ اور يَرْحَمُكَ الله “ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے اس لئے ہے کہ اس میں کاف خطاب کا ہے اور لوگوں میں یہ بول چال جاری بھی ہے، لہذ امذکورہ اصول کی بنیاد پر رُحَمُكَ الله “ کلام وَقَوْلُ أَبِى يُوسُفَ لَا يَقْوَى لِأَنَّ كَلامَ النَّاسِ فِي مُتَفَاهَمِهِمْ أَى أَهْلِ الْعُرْفِ يَتْبَعُ وُجُودَ حُرُوفِ الْهِجَاءِ وَإِفْهَامَ الْمَعْنَى وَيَتَحَقَّقُ ذَلِكَ فِي حُرُوفٍ كُلُّهَا زَوَائِدُ.
(98) Indeed, that which occurs in people's conversations is from their speech. Translation: Every speech that occurs in people's addresses, i.e., that occurs in their conversation, will be of the category of kalam al-nas. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that every speech that occurs in people's addresses, i.e., that occurs in their conversation, will be of the category of kalam al-nas. After mentioning this principle, the author of al-Hidayah proves that if a person sneezes and another person who is praying says 'yarhamukallah' in response, then the prayer of the speaker becomes invalid, because 'yarhamukallah' is of the category of kalam al-nas and kalam al-nas invalidates prayer. Therefore, this too will invalidate prayer. And 'yarhamukallah' is from the category of kalam al-nas because it contains the letter of address 'kaf' and it is current in people's conversation. So based on the mentioned principle, 'yarhamukallah' is speech. And the saying of Abu Yusuf is not strong because the speech of people according to their mutual understanding, i.e., the people of custom, depends on the existence of letters and conveying meaning, and that is realized in letters that are all extra.
Source pages p. 114

Commentary
This passage presents qawida (98) from al-Hidayah: any speech that commonly occurs in people's conversations is considered kalam al-nas. The author applies this to the case of a praying person responding to a sneeze with 'yarhamukallah'. Since this phrase is commonly used in social interactions, it qualifies as kalam al-nas and thus invalidates prayer. The text also addresses a counter-view attributed to Abu Yusuf, who reportedly did not consider such speech invalid. The commentary refutes this, asserting that the definition of kalam al-nas depends on the presence of letters and meaning, and 'yarhamukallah' includes both: it has the letter 'kaf' (you) and is meaningful. Therefore, it is mufsid. The passage emphasizes that the ruling is based on 'urf (custom) and the principle that common conversational speech is mufsid.

Key Terms
يَرْحَمُكَ الله (yarhamukallah): A common phrase meaning 'May Allah have mercy on you', said in response to a sneeze.
كلام الناس (kalam al-nas): Human speech as customarily understood, used in everyday conversation.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does saying 'yarhamukallah' during prayer invalidate it according to this principle?
A) It is a prayer (du'a) and du'a invalidates prayer.
B) It is commonly used in people's conversations and has letters and meaning.
C) It contains the word 'Allah' which is disrespectful.
D) The sneeze itself invalidates prayer.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: any utterance that is common conversational speech (kalam al-nas) invalidates prayer, even if it contains religious words.

intermediate

Teaching or Learning Speech in Prayer Invalidates
Qawida with talil and application to luqmah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule that teaching/learning speech in prayer is kalam al-nas, and apply it to distinguish between prompting one's own imam (valid) and prompting a non-imam (invalid).

Core Idea
Speech with a teaching purpose during prayer invalidates unless required to correct one's own imam.

This lesson unpacks a key principle from al-Hidayah: any speech that involves teaching or learning is considered kalam al-nas (human speech) and thus invalidates prayer. The author applies this general rule to the specific case of giving a prompt (luqmah) during prayer. A critical distinction emerges: prompting one's own imam is an exception because it serves a necessary corrective purpose, while prompting any other imam is unnecessary and constitutes teaching, hence invalid.

Guided Path
1
Identify the base rule
Locate the qawida: 'Indeed, that which contains teaching and learning is from the speech of people.' Understand that 'kalam al-nas' is speech that breaks prayer.
This is the foundational principle.
2
Analyze the application to prompting
The author applies the rule to the case of a praying person giving a prompt (luqmah) to an imam who is not his own. Why is this teaching? Because the prompter is instructing the imam.
Consider the intent and effect of the speech.
3
Understand the exception for one's own imam
Prompting one's own imam is allowed because it is a necessary correction (islah al-salah). The need exempts it from being classified as unnecessary teaching.
Necessity (hajah) changes the ruling.
4
Extract the condition 'without need'
The commentary in Tabyin al-Haqa'iq emphasizes 'li ghayr hajah' (without need). This is the operative condition. If there is need, it does not invalidate.
Conditionality is key.
5
Apply the distinction to new scenarios
Now consider: What if someone corrects his own imam but in a loud voice? What if he corrects a fellow muqtadi? Use the condition of need to rule.
Real-life application tests understanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
(۹۹) إِنَّ مَا فِيهِ تَعْلِيْمٌ وَتَعَلَّمْ فَهُوَ مِنْ كَلامِ النَّاسِ . ترجمہ: ہر وہ کلام جس میں تعلیم و تعلم ہو یعنی سیکھنا سکھانا ہو پس وہ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہے۔ تشریح: مذکورہ اصول کا مطلب یہ ہے کہ ہر ایسا کلام کرنا جس سے تعلیم یا تعلم حاصل ہوتا ہو تو وہ کلام ، کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہو گا۔ صاحب ہدایہ مذکورہ اصول کو ذکر کرنے کے بعد اس بات کو ثابت کر رہے ہیں اگر مصلی نے اپنے امام کے علاوہ کسی دوسرے امام کونماز میں لقمہ دیا تو نماز فاسد ہو جائے گی ،اس کی وجہ یہ ہے کہ اپنے امام کو لقمہ دینا تو ضرورت کی بنا پر ہے اور غیر امام کولقمہ دینا اس میں کوئی ضرورت نہیں ہے، لہذا غیر امام کولقمہ دینا یہ تعلیم وتعلم ہے، تو مذکورہ قاعدے کی بنیاد پر یہ کلام الناس کے قبیل سے ہوا اور کالام الناس مفسد صلاۃ ہے، لہذا یہ لقمہ دینا بھی غیر امام کو مفسد صلاۃ ہوگا۔ تبيين الحقائق كتاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة، ج ۱ ص ۱۵۶، ط: دار الكتاب الإسلامي] الهداية كاب الصلاة، باب ما يفسد الصلاة وما يكره فيها ، ج ١ ص ١٣٧ (قَوْلُهُ وَفَتْحُهُ عَلَى غَيْرِ إِمَامِهِ) أَى يُفْسِدُهَا لِأَنَّهُ تَعْلِيمٌ وَتَعَلَّمْ لِغَيْرِ حَاجَةٍ قَيَّدَ بِهِ لِأَنَّهُ لَوْ فَتَحَ عَلَى إمَامِهِ فَلَا فَسَادُ لِأَنَّهُ تَعَلَّقَ بِهِ إِصْلَاحُ صَلَاتِهِ.
(99) Indeed, that which contains teaching and learning is from the speech of people. Translation: Every speech that contains teaching and learning, i.e., learning and teaching, is of the category of kalam al-nas. Explanation: The meaning of the mentioned principle is that every such speech that results in teaching or learning will be of the category of kalam al-nas. The author of al-Hidayah, after mentioning this principle, proves that if a praying person prompts (luqmah) an imam other than his own imam, his prayer will become invalid. The reason is that prompting one's own imam is based on need, but prompting a non-imam is without need. Therefore, prompting a non-imam is teaching and learning, so based on the mentioned rule, it becomes from the category of kalam al-nas, and kalam al-nas invalidates prayer. Hence, prompting a non-imam also invalidates prayer. Tabyin al-Haqa'iq, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma yufsid al-salah, vol.1 p.156, Dar al-Kitab al-Islami; al-Hidayah, Kitab al-Salah, Bab ma yufsid al-salah wa ma yukrah fiha, vol.1 p.137: (His saying: 'and prompting someone other than his imam') meaning it invalidates it because it is teaching and learning without need. He restricted it to that because if he prompts his own imam, there is no invalidation, because it is connected to correcting his prayer.
Source pages p. 115

Commentary
This passage presents qawida (99) from al-Hidayah: any speech that involves teaching or learning is considered kalam al-nas, and thus invalidates prayer if said deliberately. The author applies this to the case of a praying person giving a prompt (luqmah) to an imam who is not his own imam. Such prompting is deemed unnecessary and qualifies as teaching, making it mufsid. In contrast, prompting one's own imam is permissible because it is a necessary correction to ensure the validity of the congregational prayer. The commentary from Tabyin al-Haqa'iq and al-Hidayah emphasizes the condition of 'without need' (li ghayr hajah). If there is a genuine need, such as correcting one's own imam, it does not invalidate. This distinction highlights that the ruling hinges on the presence of a valid excuse.

Key Terms
تعلیم و تعلم (ta‘lim wa ta‘allum): Teaching and learning; any speech intended to instruct or acquire knowledge.
لقمہ دینا (luqmah dena): To prompt someone by correcting or supplying words, especially during prayer.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does prompting a non-imam during prayer invalidate it according to the Hanafi school?
A) Because all speech invalidates prayer.
B) Because it is considered teaching without necessity.
C) Because it shows disrespect to the imam.
D) Because the person should remain silent and not help others.

Key Takeaway
Speech that teaches or learns during prayer invalidates unless it is a necessary correction to one's own imam.
